    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="34a8547ee6db49879c43c432ab978231">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9207238b-9160-47e2-bbfa-582c9b8bd922'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d107791835" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon, as the most promising commercial anode materials of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), has suffered from the coupling limitations on initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), capacity, and rate capability. Herein, to break such coupling limitations, sulfur-rich nitrogen-doped carbon nanomaterials (S-NC) were synthesized by a synergistic modification strategy, including structure/morphology regulation and dual heteroatom doping. The small specific surface area of S-NC is beneficial for inhibiting excessive growth of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and irreversible interfacial reaction. The covalent S can serve as active electrochemical sites by Faradaic reactions and provide extra capacity. Benefit by N, S co-doping, S-NC shows large interlayer spacing, high defects, good electronic conductivity, strong ion adsorption performance, and fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport, which combined with a more significant pore volume result in speedier reaction kinetics. Hence, S-NC possesses a high reversible specific capacity of 464.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a high ICE of 50.7%, excellent rate capability (209.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and superb long-cycle capability delivering a capacity of 229.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (85% retention) after 1800 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202300449-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202300449-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The advancement of new energy storage devices is imminent to alleviate the issues of energy crisis and global warming because of the over-exploitation and exploitation of non-renewable fossil fuels.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0001_R_d107791827e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Currently, the widely used secondary batteries are mainly lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0006_R_d107791827e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> The scarcity and high price of lithium resources limit the long-term utilization of LIBs in energy storage systems at a large scale. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as a highly potential substitute for LIBs, owing to their abundant sodium resources, cost-effectiveness, and widespread accessibility.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0013_R_d107791827e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Due to the greater atomic mass and larger ionic radius of Na compared to Li, SIBs show sluggish ion diffusion ability, leading to low capacity and slow reaction kinetics. To overcome the issues, many anode materials with high theoretical capacity like alloy-based and conversion-based materials,<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0017_R_d107791827e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> have been explored. However, the poor cycle stability of these materials due to the significant volume expansion and structural crushing leaves them far from industrialization application.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0018_R_d107791827e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Therefore, the focus of industrialization of anode materials is still on carbonaceous materials owing to their abundant resources, environmental friendliness, low price, and good chemical/electrochemical stability.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0019_R_d107791827e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, graphite, the most critical anode material for LIBs, cannot be used for SIBs due to its small layer spacing. Thus, hard carbon has become the most promising industrialized material for SIBs and occupies the vast majority of the current market for commercial anode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Although hard carbon has exhibited excellent cycling stability, there are still some difficulties for the applications to be addressed, mainly including low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), low capacity, and poor rate capability. The charge/discharge curves of hard carbon contain two regions: a sloping region in high voltage and a plateau region in low voltage, which relate to two main sodium storage mechanisms. The storage mechanisms for adsorption occur in the defect/edge sites in sloping storage, while intercalation into the graphitic layers is observed in plateau storage.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0022_R_d107791827e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Therefore, the improvement of the electrochemical performance of hard carbon mainly concentrated on the enhancement of one or two above storage modes. In general, the methods to solve the above issues of hard carbon can be categorized into two categories: (1) structure and morphology regulation,<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0025_R_d107791827e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> (2) heteroatom doping.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0028_R_d107791827e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span> The regulation of structure and morphology contains controlling graphitic structure and the morphology design. For example, increasing the degree of graphitization can increase the plateau capacity; enlarging the lattice spacing of the graphitic structure can improve the intercalation kinetics and capacity. Some physicochemical parameters, such as electronic conductivity, surface area, and porosity, can be adjusted through morphology design. Many hard carbon materials with different morphologies, including porous structures, two-dimensional nanosheets, and one-dimensional nanofibers, have been synthesized to increase the defect/edge sites.</p>
         
         <p>Heteroatom doping (such as N, S, B, and P) is an effective approach to increase the capacity and improve the electrochemical reaction kinetics of hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0029_R_d107791827e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Among them, N is the most commonly employed doping element in carbonaceous materials. N atoms are able to cause polarization in covalent structures after replacing carbon atoms, which can improve the electronic conductivity of hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0033_R_d107791827e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">11</a></sup></span> Moreover, N atoms can introduce new channels and pore size distributions and show a solid affinity for Na<sup>+</sup> ions, resulting in an excellent adsorption property to Na<sup>+</sup> ions. S doping has been reported to exhibit unique advantages in many aspects.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0034_R_d107791827e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> Firstly, S doping can effectively enlarge the layer distance of hard carbon due to the large atom size of S. Secondly, covalently bonded −C−S− plays a crucial role in realizing higher capacity Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0037_R_d107791827e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy">13</a></sup></span> Finally, S can form a −C−S−S−C− bond with a bond angle close to 90°, which induces tensile stress at the surface and significantly increases the accessible subsurface area, thereby contributing to the increase in pseudocapacitive contribution.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0038_R_d107791827e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Due to the different roles of doping elements, dual doping has been reported to be an effective doping strategy.</p>
         
         <p>Due to the complex reaction mechanism of hard carbon, a single modification method usually cannot simultaneously meet the needs of high ICE, high capacity, and good rate capability. For instance, porous hard carbon with a large surface area can realize fast reaction kinetics, thus obtaining a good rate performance. However, many exposed surfaces can lead to more solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film formation, reducing the ICE. On the contrary, highly graphitized hard carbon shows high ICE but poor rate capability. Hence, the comprehensive implementation of the aforementioned hard carbon modification techniques is imperative, which, however, still poses a substantial obstacle at present.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a multi-scale optimization strategy, including structure/morphology regulation and heteroatom doping, was adopted to synthesize sulfur-rich nitrogen-doped carbon nanomaterials (S-NC), thereby improving the overall performance of hard carbon. The as-prepared S, N co-doped hard carbon has a small specific surface area and large pore volume, reducing the generation of SEI layer and irreversible reactions and providing many ion transport channels and adsorption sites. Moreover, N doping increases the electronic conductivity and ion adsorption ability of carbon material. The introduction of S increases the interlayer spacing and defects in the carbon lattice and provides more electrochemical active sites. As a result, high ICE, high capacity, and good rate capability of S-NC have been achieved simultaneously under this synergistic mechanism.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202300449-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202300449-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The schematic illustration of the synthesis process for S-NC is shown in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0001">1</a>, which reveals a simple synthetic method that contains the calcination of polyacrylonitrile (PAN) and S powder mixture followed by the removal of excess elemental sulfur. PAN was used as C and N source. S powder is the S doping and pore-creating source for increasing the pore volume compared to NC. By this straightforward approach, N, S co-doped hard carbon has been successfully prepared. The morphologies of NC and S-NC were observed by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The S-NC (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0002">2a</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0002">b</a>) shows a similar nanostructure to NC (Figure S1), which composes of a granular structure that adheres together with a diameter of 100–200 nm, indicating that the sulfurization and dilute hydrochloric acid washing have negligible impacts on the morphologies of carbon materials. Significantly, no visible sulfur bulks can be found in S-NC, which is attributed to the fact that most of the sulfur powder can be gasified at high temperature; meanwhile, the sulfur gas atoms can diffuse into the pores of NC via capillary forces and displace the marginal pyrrolic-N of NC.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0031_R_d107791827e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0041_R_d107791827e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon">15</a></sup></span> Based on the energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings of S-NC shown in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0002">2c</a>, the homogeneous distributions of C, N, and S elements demonstrate the successful co-doping of N and S into carbon materials, and the atomic percentage of C, N, and S is 76.6 at.%, 13.42 at.%, and 8.19 at.%, respectively (Figure S2). The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of S-NC and NC were displayed in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0002">2d</a> and S1c, respectively. Both carbon materials do not show long-range ordered structures, implying the amorphous structures of NC and S-NC with a low graphitization degree. However, the short-range ordered turbostratic structure can be observed, attributed to the graphitic carbon layer. Interestingly, the distorted interlayer distance of S-NC (0.42 nm) is noticeably larger than that of NC (0.38 nm) and graphite (0.335 nm), which is due to the larger covalent radius of sulfur (102 p.m.) compared with that of nitrogen (75 p.m.) and carbon (77 p.m.). The larger interlayer distance can facilitate the insertion/extraction of sodium ions.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0041_R_d107791827e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon">15</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202300449-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b301c40e-11ad-4313-8c9e-a98540f0b2a1/asia202300449-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b301c40e-11ad-4313-8c9e-a98540f0b2a1/asia202300449-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d95b685d-02c5-4060-a69f-52c1a841bdfe/asia202300449-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b301c40e-11ad-4313-8c9e-a98540f0b2a1/asia202300449-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202300449-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202300449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The schematic illustration of the synthesis process for S-NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202300449-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e58cadd3-d9d9-43e0-8d51-fc3a60f1f043/asia202300449-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e58cadd3-d9d9-43e0-8d51-fc3a60f1f043/asia202300449-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0ed56027-c7fd-4438-94e5-719e634c71e3/asia202300449-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e58cadd3-d9d9-43e0-8d51-fc3a60f1f043/asia202300449-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202300449-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202300449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3a</a>) of NC and S-NC exhibit a broad peak at about 25° and a weak diffraction peak around 43°, corresponding to the (002) plane of the graphitic layered structure and the (101) crystal face of graphite (JCPDS No. 65-6212). This further confirms the amorphous turbostratic structures.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0042_R_d107791827e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbon">16</a></sup></span> Notably, the (002) peak of S-NC (24.9°) has a red shift compared to that of NC (25.6°), being in accordance with the expanded interlayer distance after sulfur doping. Based on Bragg's law, the <i>d</i>(002) of NC and S-NC is calculated to be 0.38 and 0.42 nm, respectively, consistent with the HRTEM measurements. The graphitization degree has an essential influence on sodium storage performance and can be evaluated by <i>R</i>-value.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0043_R_d107791827e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> The <i>R</i>-value is calculated based on the ratio of <i>B</i> to <i>A</i>, where <i>A</i> and <i>B</i> are the background and the height of (002) peak, respectively. The <i>R</i>-value of 1.0 or bigger than 1.0 illustrates an entirely disordered structure or a gradually increased graphitization degree. The <i>R</i>-value of NC and S-NC is 4.15 and 4.35, respectively, implying that the S-NC has a higher disordered structure with more active sites, contributing to sodium storage. Raman spectra can further estimate the graphitization degree. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3b</a>, both samples exhibit two prominent carbon peaks at around 1360 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The peak at 1360 cm<sup>−1</sup> is ascribed to the D band associated with the A<sub>1g</sub> mode vibrations of C<sub>6</sub> rings for disordered graphitic crystallites at the edges caused by doped N and S atoms. The peak at 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the G band related to the E<sub>2g</sub> mode phonon vibrations of two-dimensional <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup>-hybridized C−C.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0032_R_d107791827e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10c - Chem. Commun.">10c</a></sup></span> The intensity ratio of the D to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) that represents the disorder degree is calculated to be 1.53 and 1.55 for NC and S-NC, respectively, revealing high structural distortion of S-NC generated by the defects after sulfur doping,<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0046_R_d107791827e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> which is in agreement with the XRD results. A blueshift of the D and G bands is observed after introducing S, which is the feature of n-type doping caused by the electron donation of S atoms in the conjugated electron structure.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0047_R_d107791827e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Carbon">19</a></sup></span> Furthermore, compared with NC, S-NC shows a new signal at 475 cm<sup>−1</sup> that is attributed to the stretching vibration of S−S bonds,<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0048_R_d107791827e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span> indicating the covalent sulfur of S-NC.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202300449-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/183ce933-a739-49e2-b40e-256580abf774/asia202300449-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/183ce933-a739-49e2-b40e-256580abf774/asia202300449-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bcb83ed2-f36f-4cb7-ab9f-29308673e5d2/asia202300449-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/183ce933-a739-49e2-b40e-256580abf774/asia202300449-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202300449-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202300449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of NC and S-NC. (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherm curves and pore size distribution of S-NC. (d) C 1s XPS spectrum, (e) N 1s XPS spectrum, and (f) S 2p XPS spectrum of S-NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The specific surface area and pore size distribution of NC and S-NC were analyzed using nitrogen adsorption-desorption curves (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3c</a>). Both materials have type IV isotherms with H3-type hysteresis loops, showing similar Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface areas of 20.74 and 21.15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for NC and S-NC, respectively. Such BET surface area is lower than most of the reported data of hard carbon materials. The pore size distribution curve (inset of Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3c</a>) indicates the hierarchical pore structures of both materials (including micropore, mesopore, and macropore). The pore volumes of NC and S-NC are 0.104 and 0.131 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, revealing that the pore volume can be increased by introducing sulfur with less effect on the surface area. Notably, the small specific surface area of the material, on the one hand, could reduce electrolyte consumption and thus reduce the occurrence of side reactions that cause irreversible capacity, leading to high ICE. On the other hand, the small surface area can provide a large volume of energy density which is critical for the commercial development of carbon-based anodes of SIBs. Moreover, the high pore volume of S-NC with a hierarchical pore structure is beneficial for the permeation of electrolytes and the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, improving reaction kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>The dual element doped carbon skeleton was confirmed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of S-NC. As seen from Figure S3, the prominent O 1s, N 1s, C 1s, S 2s, and S 2p peaks in the XPS survey spectrum are detected, indicating the successful bonding of N and S into the carbon matrix. The atomic contents of N and S are 15.2 at.% and 5.4 at.%, respectively, which correspond with the EDS results. The high-resolution XPS spectrum of C 1s (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3d</a>) can be fitted into three peaks at 284.6, 286.1, and 289.2 eV, attributing to the C−C/C=C bond, C−N/C−S bond, and C=O/C=N bond, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0049_R_d107791827e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span> The XPS curve of N 1s (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3e</a>) can be divided into three peaks: pyridine N, pyrrolic N, and quaternary N located at 398.6, 400.4, and 402.5 eV, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0031_R_d107791827e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a></sup></span> It is suggested that nitrogen has been effectively embedded in the carbon lattice. Pyridine N can increase the charge transfer ability and electronic conductivity, while pyrrolic N can enhance the adsorption capacity and electrochemical reaction activity of hard carbon materials for SIBs. NC shows the same composition of N element as S-NC (Figure S4c). However, the ratio of pyrrolic N for NC (39.59%) is higher than that for S-NC (33.44%), indicating that the substitution of S is more inclined towards replacing pyrrolic-N to create a structure resembling thiophene.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0031_R_d107791827e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Adv. Mater.">10b</a></sup></span> Four distinct bonding modes are derived from the S 2p spectrum (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0003">3f</a>). Among them, two peaks at 163.6 and 165.1 eV correspond to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of covalent −C−S−C− bonds (thiophene S),<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0050_R_d107791827e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span> revealing the successful doping of sulfur. The peak at the higher binding energy of 167.1 eV corresponds to the sulfate species (RSO<sub>x</sub>).<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0051_R_d107791827e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span> The peak at 161.9 eV is attributed to the by-product HS<sub>x</sub>C generated during PAN vulcanization.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0050_R_d107791827e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span> The chemical structural composition of S-NC has been further characterized by Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) (Figure S5), showing the stretching vibrations of C−N bonds at 1173 cm<sup>−1</sup>, C−S bonds at 794 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and S−S bonds at 602 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0041_R_d107791827e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon">15</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0046_R_d107791827e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0052_R_d107791827e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage behavior was investigated through cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD). Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the CV curves of the S-NC electrode for the initial three cycles with a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V (<i>vs</i>. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. An irreversible cathodic peak at 1.0 V in the initial reduction is attributed to the formation of SEI film.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0032_R_d107791827e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10c - Chem. Commun.">10c</a></sup></span> The cathodic peak between 0.3 V and 0.01 V is due to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the graphitic carbon layer.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0055_R_d107791827e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a></sup></span> After the initial discharge, the electrochemical behavior of S-NC is significantly different from that of NC (Figure S6a). Two new redox couples located at 1.85/2.25 V and 1.03/1.88 V appear in the subsequent CV curves of S-NC, while NC does not have such redox peaks. This phenomenon, resembling the behavior of sodium-sulfur batteries at room temperature, is frequently found in S-doped carbon materials for SIBs and can be attributed to the gradual redox reaction of sodium ions and covalent sulfur.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0055_R_d107791827e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0056_R_d107791827e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> The redox peaks at high voltage (1.85/2.25 V) represent the solid-liquid conversion between sulfur and sodium polysulfide (Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub>, x=4–8), while the low voltage peaks (1.03/1.88 V) are due to the reduction/oxidation between sodium polysulfide and sodium sulfide (Na<sub>2</sub>S).<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0059_R_d107791827e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26c - Adv. Sci.">26c</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202300449-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e777daf-d86e-490e-8686-6bd4355d68be/asia202300449-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e777daf-d86e-490e-8686-6bd4355d68be/asia202300449-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dd3ab378-bec7-4d57-81d2-8f9ba827b9da/asia202300449-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e777daf-d86e-490e-8686-6bd4355d68be/asia202300449-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202300449-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202300449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CV curves of S-NC at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) Charge-discharge profiles of S-NC at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (c) Charge-discharge profiles of S-NC at different current densities. (d) Rate performance of the NC and S-NC electrodes at various current densities from 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> to 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (e) Long cycle performance of the NC and S-NC electrodes over 1800 cycles at the current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The GCD profiles of S-NC (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0004">4b</a>) at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> exhibit similar characteristics to the CVs with two plateaus at 1.0 V and 0.01–3.0 V in the first discharging and two pairs of redox plateaus at around 1.9/2.2 V and 1.1/1.8 V in the subsequent cycling process. The reversible redox reactions known from the overlapped CV and GCD curves after the initial cycle indicate good electrochemical stability of S-NC electrode materials. The initial discharge and charge capacity of S-NC is 917.2 and 464.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with an ICE of 50.7%. The initial capacity loss can be attributed to the SEI formation and side reactions of sodium ions and surface functional groups.<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0058_R_d107791827e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26b - Nano-Micro Lett.">26b</a></sup></span> In contrast, NC (Figure S6b) only shows a low ICE of 33.4% with a low sodiation/desodiation capacity of 620.4/207.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The high capacity and ICE value of S-NC can be attributed to the low BET surface area and S doping. The low BET surface area decreases the contact area between active materials and electrolytes, thus reducing the irreversible reactions between them. Although the BET surface area of NC is also shallow, the pore volume of NC (0.104 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) is lower than that of S-NC (0.131 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). Besides, NC displays a small interlayer distance of the graphitic carbon layer without S doping. These lead to poor electrolyte permeation and slow transport of Na<sup>+</sup> ions for NC electrodes. Moreover, the Faradaic reactions of covalent S can provide extra capacity for the S-NC electrode. All of these fulfill the high reversible capacity and ICE of the S-NC electrode.</p>
         
         <p>The rate performance of the electrode materials is demonstrated in Figures <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0004">4c</a>, <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0004">4d</a>, and S7. S-NC exhibits better rate capability than NC, showing high reversible specific capacities of 446.1, 393.8, 376.2, 344.2, 306.6, 264.3, and 209.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current densities are 0.1, 0.25, 0.5, 1.0, 2.5, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. By comparison, the discharge capacities of NC are only 209.6, 180.4, 161.8, 144.1, 125.8, 112.1, and 88.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the same current densities. When the current density is restored to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity of S-NC could recover to 426.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, while only a low capacity of 205.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved for NC. Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0004">4e</a> shows long-term cycle stability measurements of S-NC and NC. S-NC exhibits excellent long-cycle stability with 85% capacity retention after 1800 cycles at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The CE value in the initial cycle is calculated to be 85.3% and maintains more than 99% in the following cycles, resulting in a high capacity of 229 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after cycles. In contrast, NC only reserves a low discharge capacity of 135.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1800 cycles. The GCD curves (Figure S8) during cycling further indicate the high stability of S-NC and NC electrodes. The S, N co-doped carbon materials in this work show tremendous rate and cycle performance compared to the previously reported non-metallic element-doped carbon materials for SIBs, as demonstrated in Table S1.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted to investigate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the electrode materials. Before cycles, the S-NC electrode has smaller charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and higher electronic conductivity compared with the NC electrode due to the shorter semicircle size in the Nyquist plots (Figure S9), which can be attributed to the expanded interlayer distance and N, S-doping- induced defects. To reveal the sodium ion diffusion ability, EIS tests under different charging and discharging cut-off voltages (D1.5 V, D0.3 V, D0.01 V, CH0.3 V, CH1.5 V) during the second cycle have been performed. The Nyquist plots of NC and S-NC are shown in Figures S10b and 5a. The low-frequency region revealed the presence of Warburg impedance, which corresponds to the diffusion of electrolyte ions. The calculated Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i>) based on the <i>Z</i>′-<i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> plots for NC and S-NC are illustrated in Figures S10c and 5b. The <i>σ</i> values of NC at D1.5 V, D0.3 V, D0.01 V, CH0.3 V, and CH1.5 V are fitted to be 99.65, 28.08, 11.17, 24.96, and 45.92 ohm s<sup>−1/2</sup>, respectively; and that for S-NC are 6.83, 14.34, 4.09, 12.06, and 6.19 ohm s<sup>−1/2</sup>, respectively. The sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) could be calculated by <i>σ</i>, and its calculation formula is as follow:<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0060_R_d107791827e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Energy Lett.">27</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="asia202300449-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/b85ef481-5a25-451f-ab2f-4a230687916a/asia202300449-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values of NC and S-NC at different cut-off voltages are shown in Table S2. S-NC delivers a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values than NC, indicating improved ion diffusion kinetics of S-NC through N, S co-doping.</p>
         
         <p>In the high-frequency region, the electrochemical impedance of both NC and S-NC exhibits a trend of initially increasing and then decreasing, which is mainly attributed to the formation and decomposition of SEI film during discharging/charging. The fitted results of the Nyquist plots (Table S3) indicate that NC overall has higher <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> than S-NC, indicating the enhancement of electronic conductivity by S doping. By contrary, S-NC shows lower SEI impedance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) than NC at different cut-off voltages. Importantly, the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of NC at D1.5 V and CH1.5 V are extremely low, revealing that most SEI decomposes during charge process. In contrast, the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of S-NC at D1.5 V and CH1.5 V are 9.44 and 11.29 Ω, respectivly, meaning that the SEI would not decompose completely, a thin and robust SEI film can be preserved after charging due to S doping.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The CV curves (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0005">5c</a> and S11a) of S-NC and NC at different scan rates (0.1–5.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) reveal the Na storage kinetics. The capacity contribution can be divided into capacitive Na<sup>+</sup> storage and diffusion-controlled Na<sup>+</sup> storage. In general, the current (<i>i</i>, mA) and the relative scan rate (<i>ν</i>, mV s<sup>−1</sup>) can be fitted by the following equation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="asia202300449-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/f685b33d-a09e-4655-a023-3e3c56bce74b/asia202300449-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202300449-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a61e1f8-1439-4111-b2ac-f4d2ba2c3172/asia202300449-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a61e1f8-1439-4111-b2ac-f4d2ba2c3172/asia202300449-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/490f3c66-cc2b-4ecb-8627-d0560b1f86a9/asia202300449-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a61e1f8-1439-4111-b2ac-f4d2ba2c3172/asia202300449-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202300449-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202300449" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Nyquist plots under different charging and discharging cut-off voltages during the second cycle and (b) calculation of the Warburg coefficient based on the <i>Z</i>′-<i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> plot in the low-frequency region for S-NC. (c) CV curves of S-NC at different sweep rates from 0.1 to 5.0 mV ⋅ s<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Relationship between log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>v</i>) derived from the CV curves in (c). (e) The activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of NC and S-NC. (f) The calculated sodium diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) of NC and S-NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">where a is a constant and <i>b</i> reflects the sodium storage behavior: when <i>b</i> is equal to 1.0, the capacity contribution is the surface pseudocapacitance; when <i>b</i> is equal to 0.5, the electrochemical reaction is dominated by diffusion control. For the NC electrode (Figure S11b), the <i>b</i>-value of peak A is fitted to be 1.05, revealing a surface capacitive controlled process; the low <i>b</i>-value of 0.73 for peak C indicates both capacitive and diffusion-controlled reactions exist simultaneously. For S-NC electrode (Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0005">5d</a>), the <i>b</i>-values of peaks A2, A3, C1, C2, and C3 are 0.93, 0.96, 0.91, 0.95, and 0.73, respectively. In addition to the peaks (A3, C3) those are similar to NC, the <i>b</i>-values of the new peaks (A2, C1, C2) in S-NC are all close to 1.0, suggesting that the redox reaction mechanism of covalent S is a surface pseudocapacitive storage. To further quantify the pseudocapacitive contributions of Na<sup>+</sup> ions to the current response (<i>i</i>) at a fixed potential (<i>V</i>), the following expression was presented:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="asia202300449-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/e83ac218-04cc-46f5-a48d-f185f317b0fe/asia202300449-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:18614728:media:asia202300449:asia202300449-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are the coefficients of surface-driven pseudocapacitance and diffusion-controlled contributions, respectively, and <i>v</i> are the scanning speeds. As displayed in Figure S12, the pseudocapacitance contribution ratio of S-NC expand from 60% to 89% with enlarging the scanning speeds from 0.1 to 2.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is larger than that of NC (52% to 81%) at all scan rates. The high capacitive storage contribution of the S-NC electrode reveals fast electrochemical reaction kinetics due to the Faradaic reaction of S-containing covalent bonds.</p>
         
         <p>Figure S13 shows the EIS data at various temperatures from 293.15 K to 333.15 K. The activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) can be evaluated by fitting Nyquist plots based on the Arrhenius law to estimate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport kinetics. As shown in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0005">5e</a>, S-NC exhibits a lower activation energy of the charge transfer process (<i>E</i><sub>a1</sub>=30.33 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) than NC (30.33 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). Further, the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a2</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI of NC (41.86 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is much larger than that of S-NC (50.55 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). The results suggest improved kinetics of S-NC for both charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport across the SEI benefited by the S doping.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was studied. Figure S14 shows the GITT profiles of the two electrodes. The variation in voltage over time is used to determine transport ability of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is obtained by applying the following formula:<span><sup><a href="#asia202300449-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202300449-bib-0061_R_d107791827e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>where <i>τ</i> refers to the duration of the current pulse (s), and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> correspond to the mass (g), molar mass (mol), and molar volume (cm<sup>3</sup> ⋅ mol<sup>−1</sup>) of the active material. Additionally, S denotes the electrode-electrolyte interfacial area (cm<sup>2</sup>), Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> represents the steady-state potential change resulting from the current pulse (V), and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> refers to the change in the overall voltage under galvanic conditions, excluding the IR drop (V). As shown in Figure <a href="#asia202300449-fig-0005">5f</a>, the S-NC electrode exhibits a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> value than the NC electrode, indicating that S-NC has a faster Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics than NC.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202300449-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202300449-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have proposed a reasonable modification of hard carbon to alleviate the contradiction between initial Coulombic efficiency, capacity, and rate capability by controlling the structure and morphology and dual doping with N, S. The as-synthesized sulfur-rich nitrogen-doped carbon nanomaterials (S-NC) exhibit several outstanding merits in terms of structural characteristics, including (1) low BET surface area decreases the contact area between the active material and the electrolyte, thus decreases the irreversible capacity loss caused by excess SEI formation and side reaction; (2) N doping boosts electronic conductivity and adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions on the surface; (3) expanded lattice spacing and high defects caused by big size S atom doping and the significant pore volume lead to a faster ion transfer efficiency and more reaction active sites; (4) the Faradaic reaction of S-containing covalent bonds increases the pseudocapacitive storage and reaction kinetics as well. Accordingly, S-NC shows a high ICE (50.7%), high reversible capacity (464.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent rate performance (209.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) simultaneously. This study presents novel concepts aimed at overcoming the coupling constraint that hinders the electrochemical efficacy of hard carbon.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202300449-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202300449-sec-0004-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia202300449-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia202300449-sec-0005-title"> Chemicals</h3>
            
            <p>Polyacrylonitrile (PAN, <i>M</i><sub>W</sub>=150000) and sulfur powder were analytical grade, used without further purification, and supplied by Macklin Co., Ltd. Hydrochloric acid (37 wt.%) was supplied by Macklin Co., Ltd. Super P conductive carbon black, polyvinyl difluoride (PVDF), and n-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) were analytical grade, used without further purification, and supplied by Canrd Co., Ltd. Copper foil, aluminium foil, sodium foil, and electrolyte consisting of 1.0 mol L<sup>−1</sup> sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) in ethyl carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1 : 1 in volume ratio) with 5 vol.% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) were supplied by Canrd Co., Ltd.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia202300449-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia202300449-sec-0006-title"> Material Synthesis</h3>
            
            <p><i>Synthesis of the sulfur-rich and nitrogen co-doped carbon nanoparticles (S-NC)</i>: Firstly, 2.0 g of PAN and 2.0 g of sulfur powder were put into an agate mortar and milled for 1 h. Subsequently, the mixture was transferred into a porcelain boat and calcinated in a tube furnace at 600 °C for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> under nitrogen atmosphere with a flow rate of 150 sccm. Finally, the samples were added to 5 mol L<sup>−1</sup> dilute hydrochloric acid with stirring for 3 h at 60 °C, followed by filtering and washing with deionized water to neutrality and drying in vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h.</p>
            
            <p><i>Synthesis of nitrogen-doped carbon nanoparticles (NC)</i>: the NC was prepared by a similar process of SN-C except for the addition of sulfur powder in the first step and the hydrochloric acid washing step.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia202300449-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia202300449-sec-0007-title"> Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The morphologies and crystal sizes of samples were examined using field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, JEOL JSM-7800F) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL JEM-2100) at an operating voltage of 15 and 120 kV, respectively. The microstructures and elemental compositions of products were characterized utilizing high-resolution TEM (HRTRM, the operating voltage is 200 kV), and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) attached to TEM. The crystalline structures of samples were studied by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8 Advance) with Cu K<i>α</i> radiation in a 2-theta range of 10–60° at a scan rate of 6° min<sup>−1</sup>. The graphitization degree of carbon materials was analyzed by Raman spectra (Thermo Fisher DXR) with a 532 nm laser. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms were investigated by a gas adsorption analyzer (Micromeritics Tristar II 3020) with a pretreatment temperature of 150 °C. The specific surface area (SSA) and pore size distribution was calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method and Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model, respectively. The surface compositions and structures of products were determined by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Fisher Nexsa G2) with Al K<i>α</i> radiation. The functional groups of the samples were analyzed by Fourier Transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR, Thermo Fisher Scientific Nicolet IN10) within 400–2800 cm<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia202300449-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia202300449-sec-0008-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation process of working electrodes was as follows: firstly, 80 wt.% active materials, 10 wt.% super P conductive carbon black, and 10 wt.% PVDF binder were mixed in NMP with stirring for 1 h; secondly, the obtained slurry was blade coated on the copper foil current collector, followed by drying in vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h; finally, the copper foil was subjected to roll-pressing at a pressure of 10 MPa and subsequently punched into 12 mm diameter disks. The active material was loaded at a mass of approximately 1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Half cells in the form of CR2032 coin-type were assembled inside an argon-filled glovebox (Mikrouna Super), utilizing sodium slices, glass fibers (Whatman GF/D), and 1.0 mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC and DEC (1 : 1 in volume ratio) with 5 vol.% FEC as the counter electrodes, electrolyte, and separators, respectively. The Neware battery testing system (CT-4008) was employed to conduct galvanostatic charging/discharging (GCD) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests, where the GCD was collected in a potential range of 0.01–3.0 V from 0.1 to 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The GITT was conducted with a pulse time of 10 min and a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a rest interval of 1 h between each pulse. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed using a Gamry electrochemical workstation (Interface 1000E). The CV was measured within a potential window of 0.01–3.0 V at various scan rates ranging from 0.1–5.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, while the EIS was carried out with an AC perturbation of 10 mV and in a frequency range of 100 kHz–10 mHz.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
